Not because of the number — because of what it represented. Eighteen hours of work you couldn't account for. Deliverables that arrived sideways. A revision cycle that had no beginning and no end, just a contractor who seemed busy and a project that seemed to be happening and a feeling in your gut that something was off. You paid it anyway.\\n\\nThat feeling is data. Your time has a real dollar value. Your reputation has a real dollar value. The work that doesn't get done because you're babysitting someone who should be delivering has a real dollar value. Somewhere in your business right now, you're subsidizing a bad hire with your own labor, your own credibility, and your own sleep. Most founders do this math wrong — they count the direct cost of the contractor and ignore everything else. That's how you end up stuck, paying twice: once for the work, and once for the mess you can't quite bring yourself to clean up.\\n\\n## H2.1 — The Decision Guide\\n\\nMost founders already know something is wrong. The harder question is whether firing is the right move, and if it is, how to do it without destroying what you spent months building. The stakes are higher than most founders realize: a botched contractor ending can cost more in lost work, legal exposure, and team morale than the original hire ever did. That is the hidden math most people skip. Working through this decision means you are already ahead of the curve.\\n\\n### H3.1: The damage a bad ending causes\\n\\nMost founders think firing is a discrete event. You tell them it is over, you stop paying them, and the problem goes away. It does not work that way. When a contractor leaves badly, they take institutional knowledge with them: access to accounts, context about your systems, relationships with your clients or vendors. That knowledge transfer gap can stall projects for weeks or months, and the replacement cost compounds fast. The real damage is the hidden blast radius, not the headline event.\\n\\nFurthermore, your existing team watches how you handle exits. Word travels fast in remote work environments, and contractor networks are tight. A reputation for burning bridges makes future hiring harder, and it signals to current team members that they too could be discarded badly. What you do to one contractor echoes in the minds of everyone watching. Morale erosion is invisible at first, but it shows up in output long before you connect it to the incident. This is the damage most people do not see coming.\\n\\n### H3.2: The threshold — when firing is genuinely worth it\\n\\nTo begin with, the question is not whether the contractor is bad. The question is whether the cost of their continued presence exceeds the cost of their exit. That threshold is crossed when their work is actively degrading your product, your client relationships, or your team culture in ways that are not recoverable through coaching or redirection. A contractor who misses deadlines is annoying; one whose misses cause your clients to churn is a different equation entirely. You need to do the math explicitly, not just feel it. Name the cost in hours, reputation, and downstream impact before you make the call.\\n\\nConsequently, performance is only half the equation. A contractor who is trying, who is coachable, who is honest about their limitations, is salvageable in ways that someone who is defensive, dishonest, or actively covering up problems is not. Ask yourself: is this person going to get better with feedback, or are they defending the status quo? If the answer is the latter, you are not firing a bad performer. You are firing a closed learning loop. That distinction matters because it changes both your urgency and your process. The threshold is crossed when the trajectory is downward and resistant to correction.\\n\\n### H3.3: The hesitant reader — maybe don't fire, here's why\\n\\nWorse, founders often fire too fast. The friction you feel is real, but it is not always proportional to the problem. Remote contractors often underperform not because they are incapable, but because they lack context, unclear direction, or inadequate tooling. Those are solvable problems. Before you fire anyone, ask whether the failure is in the person or in the system you put them in. If it is the system, you owe it to yourself to fix the system first.\\n\\n[91 more lines in file. Wait for approval before Phase 2.\\n\\n---\\n\\n### Phase 2 — H2 Structure Proposal\\n\\n**Input:** Approved H1\\n\\n**Output:** H2 section headers that map the article's argument arc\\n\\nEach H2 is a stage in the reader's journey from problem to solution. The H2s are not topics — they are rhetorical moves.\\n\\n**Structure types:**\\n- **Problem → Disruption → Solution:** H2.1 names the false belief, H2.2 dismantles it, H2.3+ provides the right answer\\n- **Diagnostic → Evidence → Resolution:** H2.1 explains the mechanism, H2.2 shows the evidence (case study, data), H2.3 gives the prescription\\n- **Setup → Conflict → Resolution:** H2.1 establishes context, H2.2 introduces the tension, H2.3 resolves it\\n\\nPresent H2 structure with brief rationale. Each H3 has a one-sentence main idea stating what that section proves or establishes.\\n\\n**Rules:**\\n- Each H3 = one rhetorical move (one proof, one disruption, one example)\\n- H3s within an H2 should be parallel in type — if H3.1 is a mechanism, H3.2 should also be a mechanism, not a case study\\n- Main ideas are complete sentences. \\\"Why the geographic patchwork makes protection feel absolute when it isn't\\\" — that is not a main idea. \\\"The non-compete feels solid because legal language creates the impression of proportional protection, but jurisdiction determines enforceability, not contract quality\\\" — that is a main idea.\\n\\nPresent H3 structure. Wait for approval before proceeding.\\n\\n---\\n\\n## Critical Rules\\n\\n### Phase Gates Are Non-Negotiable\\n\\nEach phase requires explicit approval before the next begins. Skipping or abbreviating phases produces structurally weak content. The editor reviews each phase. Vague rejections are not helpful — state exactly what needs to change and why.\\n\\n### Writer Role\\n\\nThe writer (human or AI) generates per phase specs. The writer waits for approval. This is what the paragraph proves or argues.\\n- **Evidence**: Support the main idea with specific proof — stats, examples, mechanism details, test items, data. Do not just describe the concept; give the specific contents.\\n- **Analysis**: Explain *why* the evidence matters — the causal mechanism, the implication for the reader's situation. Show how the evidence leads to the conclusion.\\n- **Link/Transition**: End by signaling what the next paragraph will address, or by implicating the reader's next question.\\n\\n**What each step prevents:**\\n- Main missing → paragraph has no controlling idea, drifts\\n- Evidence missing → paragraph announces rather than proves (three sentences doing the same job = evidence is missing)\\n- Analysis missing → paragraph states consequences without explaining why they follow\\n- Link missing → paragraph ends as a dead end, next paragraph feels disconnected\\n\\n**Example (good MEAL application):**\\n> Main: \\\"Non-competes are enforcement instruments, not ownership instruments.\\\"\\n> Evidence: \\\"They restrict where a contractor can work after the engagement ends — a geographic exclusion that activates only at termination.\\\"\\n> Analysis: \\\"If the contractor never terminates — if they simply add clients — the clause never activates. You paid for a safety net that requires the exact situation you wanted to avoid in order to function.\\\"\\n> Link: \\\"This is why the first thing most owners get wrong about what they bought.\\\"\\n\\n**What NOT to touch:**\\n- H2 and H3 headings (write around them, they stay as-is)\\n- H2 intro paragraphs (leave them alone in this step)\\n- The FAQ section (leave it alone in this step)\\n- The Bottom Line / conclusion (leave it alone in this step)\\n- The opening story (leave it exactly as provided)\\n\\n**Rules for prose:**\\n- 4-6 sentences per paragraph\\n- Every paragraph (except first in a section) opens with a transition word\\n- No em-dashes, no contractions\\n- No bold in body text\\n- Primary keyword appears in H1, first paragraph, at least one H2, and conclusion\\n- \\\"Perhaps\\\" hedge only on counterfactual causal claims — never on analytical claims\\n- No Chinese characters or any other language leaks in English content\\n\\n**Transition word library (by function):**\\n- Adding evidence: Furthermore, Moreover, In addition\\n- Contrast/concession: Yet, Nevertheless, Still, Despite this\\n- Causality: Consequently, Therefore, As a result\\n- Intensification: Worse, Beyond, Even more\\n- Example/illustration: For instance, Consider, Take the case of\\n- Concession: Although, Even if, Granted\\n\\n**Output:** Write to `awaa-output/phase-5-full-draft.md`. Respond with only `[File written]: /path.md @aw`.\\n\\n---\\n\\n### Step 2 — Revise H2 Intro Paragraphs\\n\\n**What to do:**\\nRead each H2 section. Check that the H2 intro paragraph accurately represents what the section actually covers. The intro should set up the argument arc of the section — not just introduce the topic broadly.\\n\\n**What to check:**\\n- Does the intro reference the specific mechanism or evidence discussed in the H2?\\n- Does the intro create logical momentum toward the H3 arguments?\\n- Is the \\\"because\\\" or \\\"so\\\" or \\\"yet\\\" relationship between the intro and the section content clear?\\n- Does the intro use specific language from the section, not generic summaries?\\n\\n**What NOT to do:**\\n- Do not turn the intro into a summary of everything in the section\\n- Do not list the H3 topics in the intro — that destroys the discoverability of the structure\\n- The intro is a loading dock — it gets the reader onto the right floor, not a map of the whole building\\n\\n**If the intro needs revision:**\\nRevise it directly. Then write the revised version to the file.\\n\\n---\\n\\n### Step 3 — Check Transitions Between Paragraphs\\n\\n**What to do:**\\nUse the transitions-skill (`transitions-skill/SKILL.md`) to audit and fix paragraph-to-paragraph transitions. This step is critical — transitions are not just \\\"add a transition word at the start.\\\" The transition must carry the logical thread from one paragraph to the next.\\n\\n**What to check:**\\n- Does the first sentence of each paragraph (after the first in a section) connect to the last sentence of the previous paragraph?\\n- Does each paragraph end with a pivot sentence that implicates reader agency?\\n- Does vocabulary at paragraph boundaries avoid repetition of the same word family?\\n- Do H2 intros transition correctly from the previous section's closing argument?\\n\\n**See:** `transitions-skill/SKILL.md` for the full method.\\n\\n---\\n\\n### Step 4 — Check FAQs Against the Article\\n\\n**What to do:**\\nRead each FAQ question. Verify that the answer can actually be derived from the article's content — not just the topic area, but the specific argument made. If a question asks about something the article only touches on briefly, revise the question to match what the article actually proves.\\n\\n**What to check:**\\n- FAQ 1: Does the article actually explain the state/jurisdiction rule? Does the answer cover the contractor-living-in-a-different-state scenario?\\n- FAQ 2: Does the article actually explain IP assignment defaults? Does the answer cover what happens without an explicit assignment clause?\\n- FAQ 3: Does the article actually provide the checklist items? Would a reader know what to look for after reading?\\n\\n**What to avoid:**\\n- FAQ questions that are answerable from general knowledge, not from the article's specific argument\\n- Questions that cover topics the article doesn't actually prove — don't ask about something you didn't establish\\n- Recycled versions of questions the reader already has from the opening story\\n\\n**Revision:** If a question doesn't match the article's argument, revise the question to match. Then answer it based on what the article actually says.\\n\\n---\\n\\n## Output Format\\n\\n**File:** `awaa-output/phase-5-full-draft.md`\\n\\n**Structure:**\\n```\\n[META BLOCK — title, meta description, primary keyword, secondary keywords]\\n\\n[OPENING STORY — do not touch, do not modify, insert as provided]\\n\\n## H2.1 — Section Title\\n[H2 intro paragraph — may be revised in Step 2]\\n\\n### H3.1.1 — Subheading\\n[Paragraph 1 — converted from canvas]\\n[Paragraph 2 — converted from canvas]\\n\\n### H3.1.2 — Subheading\\n[Paragraph 1]\\n[Paragraph 2]\\n\\n[Callout quote for H2.1 section]\\n\\n## H2.2 — Section Title\\n[H2 intro paragraph]\\n[...H3 sections with paragraphs...]\\n[Callout quote]\\n\\n[FAQ section — revised in Step 4]\\n\\n[BOTTOM LINE — conclusion, do not touch unless Step 4 requires FAQ-driven revision]\\n```\\n\\n---\\n\\n## Quality Checklist\\n\\nBefore responding with `@aw`:\\n- [ ] All H3 subheadings present and match canvas\\n- [ ] All paragraphs are 4-6 sentences\\n- [ ] All paragraphs (except first in section) open with a transition word\\n- [ ] No em-dashes, no contractions\\n- [ ] No Chinese characters or other language leaks\\n- [ ] \\\"Perhaps\\\" used only on counterfactual causal claims\\n- [ ] Primary keyword appears in H1, first paragraph, at least one H2, conclusion\\n- [ ] H2 intros represent section content (Step 2 done)\\n- [ ] Paragraph transitions carry logical thread (Step 3 done)\\n- [ ] FAQ questions match article argument (Step 4 done)\\n- [ ] Opening story unchanged\\n\"}],\"isError\":false,\"timestamp\":1776004958375}}\r\n{\"type\":\"message\",\"id\":\"52ab3d11\",\"parentId\":\"fde11c21\",\"timestamp\":\"2026-04-12T14:42:57.337Z\",\"message\":{\"role\":\"assistant\",\"content\":[{\"type\":\"text\",\"text\":\"Good.
